        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '2acd4732-cd43-4591-9db7-241d3054db2e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d76141131" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>As one of the most promising cathodes for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), O3-type layered transition metal oxides commonly suffer from inevitably complicated phase transitions and sluggish kinetics. Here, a Na[Li<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode material with the exposed {010} active facets by multiple-layer oriented stacking nanosheets is presented. Owing to reasonable geometrical structure design and chemical substitution, the electrode delivers outstanding rate performance (71.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 16.9 kW kg<sup>−1</sup> at 50C), remarkable cycling stability (91.9% capacity retention after 600 cycles at 5C), and excellent compatibility with hard carbon anode. Based on the combined analyses of cyclic voltammograms, ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy, and operando X-ray diffraction, the reaction mechanisms behind the superior electrochemical performance are clearly articulated. Surprisingly, Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox couples are simultaneously involved in the charge compensation with a highly reversible O3–P3 phase transition during charge/discharge process and the Na<sup>+</sup> storage is governed by a capacitive mechanism via quantitative kinetics analysis. This optimal bifunctional regulation strategy may offer new insights into the rational design of high-performance cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201803765-sec-0010">
         
         <p>In order to integrate a variety of intermittent renewable and clean energy (wind, solar, and wave) into the electrical grid to avoid the risks of fossil fuels related environmental pollution, large-scale energy storage technology has received significant attention over the past decade. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as one of the most promising next-generation batteries for grid-scale storage applications because of the abundance and wide distribution of sodium as well as the similar chemical properties to that of lithium-ion batteries.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0001_R_d76141122e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0004_R_d76141122e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Many efforts have been devoted toward seeking novel Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion compounds to realize high-performance SIBs.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0005_R_d76141122e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Commun.">5</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0009_R_d76141122e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></span> Among the various available cathode materials, O3-type layered oxides, NaTMO<sub>2</sub> (where TM refers to transition metal), are considered to be one of the most fascinating candidates due to their sufficient sodium-ion reservoirs (an important factor in full cell system), high-energy density, and simple synthesis process.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0010_R_d76141122e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0013_R_d76141122e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a></span> Nevertheless, the frequent multiple phase transformations and sluggish kinetics in O3-type cathodes are prone to result in rapid capacity decline and deteriorated rate capability. To address this problem, chemical substitutions (replacing TM atoms in the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs) such as Ti-substituted NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Fe-substituted NaFe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Cu and Ti co-substituted NaNi<sub>0.45</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> have been demonstrated as efficient strategies to restrain the unfavorable multiphase transformation and reduce several voltage plateaus.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0014_R_d76141122e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0016_R_d76141122e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></span> Besides, the incorporation of Li and Mg into the transition metal layer can improve the structural stability and obtain better electrochemical performance.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0017_R_d76141122e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#adma201803765-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0018_R_d76141122e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></span> Some pioneers have confirmed that reasonable geometrical structure is also of great importance to improve Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0019_R_d76141122e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0022_R_d76141122e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></span> In this case, an optimal bifunctional modulation combining the advantages of chemical substitution and structure design might realize both excellent rate capability and long cycle stability. Meanwhile, understanding the inherent reaction mechanisms in electrode materials during charge/discharge process could provide significant guidelines for precisely designing high-performance cathode materials for SIBs.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0023_R_d76141122e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Chem.">23</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0026_R_d76141122e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Hence, we design an O3-type Na[Li<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode material with the exposed {010} active facets by multiple-layer oriented stacking nanosheets through reasonable structure design and chemical substitution. This electrode delivers remarkable capacity retention of 91.9% after 600 cycles at 5C, an excellent specific capacity of 71.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50C, and outstanding sodium-ion full battery performance when paired with hard carbon anode. Together with reaction mechanism elucidation by ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and operando X-ray diffraction (XRD), cationic nickel and copper are involved in the charge compensation mechanism with a highly reversible O3–P3 phase transition upon Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation/intercalation. It is worth noting that a capacitance-dominated Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism is revealed by quantitative transport kinetics analysis. The superior cathode material with outstanding electrochemical performance will be a milestone for the large-scale application of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and O3-Na[Li<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials (hereafter denoted as O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM, respectively) were prepared via thermal polymerization method followed by high-temperature annealing. To directly record the formation process of O3-NaLNMCM material during the calcination process, scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and in situ XRD at different temperatures were executed (Figures S1 and S2, Supporting Information). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0001">1</a>a,b, XRD patterns of O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM are ascribed to the O3-type α-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> layered structure with rhombohedral R<img alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201803765:adma201803765-math-0001" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/96b57d04-4bce-44da-a398-2a5bb3d019d0/adma201803765-math-0001.png">m space group.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0013_R_d76141122e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a>, <a href="#adma201803765-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0014_R_d76141122e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></span> Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0001">1</a>c displays that the crystal structures of typical O3-type Na-based layered oxides (view along <i>b</i> axis and <i>c</i> axis) are composed of alternately stacking of edge sharing TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedral layers and Na ions layers. The observed pattern of O3-NaLNMCM matches well with the calculated pattern (<i>R</i><sub>p</sub> 6.48% and <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> 10.81%) and the detailed crystallographic parameters refined by the Rietveld method are summarized in Tables S1 and S2 in the Supporting Information. It can be seen that Li, Cu, and Mg elements localize at the octahedral 3b Wyckoff sites due to the similar ionic radius with that of nickel element, suggesting a uniform incorporation into the transition metal layer. The morphological and structural features of O3-NaLNMCM were characterized by SEM and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0001">1</a>d,e). The secondary particles consist of multiple-layer oriented stacking nanosheets (an average thickness around 17.8 nm, Figure S3, Supporting Information) with sharp corners and edges. In contrast, as displayed in Figures S4 and S5 in the Supporting Information, the morphology of O3-NaNM is a brick-like structure and the thicknesses (around 205.8 nm) of {010} active facets are lower than that of O3-NaLNMCM (around 384.7 nm). It is worth noting that the increased thickness of {010} active facets of O3-NaLNMCM could provide more channels for the fast and efficient transportation of Na<sup>+</sup>, resulting in superior electrochemical performance.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0027_R_d76141122e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0029_R_d76141122e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></span> Moreover, the unique multiple-layer oriented stacking nanosheet structure could greatly enhance the rate performance because of the short diffusion distances and increased electrode–electrolyte contact area. Meanwhile, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0001">1</a>f,g, the detailed structure information of O3-NaLNMCM was further confirmed by high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) with the fast Fourier transform (FFT) pattern as insert and selected area electron diffraction (SAED). The distance of ≈2.9 Å between neighboring (001) lattice fringes is consistent with the value of cell parameter <i>a</i>. The HR-TEM image with corresponding line profiles (Figure S6, Supporting Information) demonstrates that the TM atoms arrange in a hexagonal symmetry when viewed along the [001] crystallographic direction, which is in good agreement with the results of FFT pattern and SAED. Energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) and secondary ion mass spectroscopy mapping indicate that the all elements are uniformly distributed in the O3-NaLNMCM particle (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0001">1</a>h–m and Figure S7, Supporting Information). In addition, as plotted in Table S3 in the Supporting Information, the chemical compositions of O3-NaLNMCM were analyzed by means of inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) and corresponding results are highly consistent with the designed theoretical value.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201803765-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a756fa1d-e809-4b91-bcc4-3664797f7391/adma201803765-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a756fa1d-e809-4b91-bcc4-3664797f7391/adma201803765-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3347d1af-f8ed-4015-ab28-02c0b6ad6edd/adma201803765-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a756fa1d-e809-4b91-bcc4-3664797f7391/adma201803765-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201803765-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201803765" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure of O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM materials. a,b) Powder XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement plots of O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM materials. c) O3-type crystal structure viewed along <i>b</i> axis (left) and <i>c</i> axis (right). d) SEM image, e) TEM image, f) HR-TEM image with FFT pattern as insert, g) SAED image, and h–m) EDS maps of O3-NaLNMCM material.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM electrodes were tested in Na half cells (1C = 125 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the O3-NaNM electrode obviously shows existence of several voltage plateaus at 0.5C in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>a, which reflect multiphase transformations of O3<sub>hex.</sub>−O′3<sub>mon.</sub>−P3<sub>hex.</sub>−P′3<sub>mon.</sub>−P3′′<sub>hex.</sub> according to the previous study.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0014_R_d76141122e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma201803765-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0015_R_d76141122e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a></span> After chemical substitution, the charge/discharge curves of O3-NaLNMCM electrode become smoother and three voltage plateaus above 2.6 V disappeared (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>b). Meanwhile, O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM electrodes were investigated by cyclic voltammograms (CV) at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>c,d) and the results of O3-NaNM electrode indicate four pairs of redox peaks located at 2.76/2.53, 3.25/3.20, 3.53/3.47, and 3.66/3.57 V, respectively, which is consistent with the four voltage plateaus in galvanostatic charge/discharge curves. In marked contrast to the O3-NaNM electrode, O3-NaLNMCM electrode only shows one obvious broad oxidation/reduction peak at 2.99 and 2.51 V, which resembles the properties of capacitors. The vanished three peaks in the high voltage region indicate that the complicated phase transformations are successfully restrained due to the suppression of TMO<sub>2</sub> gliding after substituted elements are uniformly being incorporated into the crystal lattice. To further evaluate the rate performance, O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM electrodes were tested at different current densities ranging from 0.5 to 50C (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>e–h). As expected, the O3-NaLNMCM electrode delivers a much better rate capability with more stable specific capacity at each rate and corresponding detailed electrochemical parameters are comprehensively summarized in Table S4 in the Supporting Information. Surprisingly, O3-NaLNMCM electrode can still exhibit 71.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at an ultrahigh rate of 50C (6.25 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and the corresponding power density up to 16.9 kW kg<sup>−1</sup>, whereas the specific capacity of O3-NaNM electrode is merely 10.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density comes back to 0.5C after various rate tests, the discharge capacity of the O3-NaLNMCM electrode could be recovered to 126.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than that of O3-NaNM electrode, demonstrating the excellent reversibility of the O3-NaLNMCM cathode material (Figure S8, Supporting Information). Moreover, the greatly improved Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics of the O3-NaLNMCM electrode are confirmed by the higher Na<sup>+</sup> apparent diffusion coefficient, smoother charge/discharge curves, better capacity retention, and lower midpoint voltage decline (Figures S9–S12, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>i, CV curves of O3-NaLNMCM electrode at various sweeping rates were recorded to further reveal the reasons behind the outstanding rate performance, and the reaction mechanism is demonstrated by the quantitative kinetics analysis.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0030_R_d76141122e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Lett.">30</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0032_R_d76141122e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></span> According to the equation <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, the <i>b</i> parameters of the oxidation and reduction peaks (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>j) are determined as 0.912 and 0.906, respectively, which manifest that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage is governed by a capacitive mechanism. The capacitive contribution can be derived via the following equation: <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> (where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the capacitive current and the diffusion controlled current, respectively).<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0033_R_d76141122e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#adma201803765-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0034_R_d76141122e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochim. Acta">34</a></span> For example, ≈71% of the total capacity is provided by capacitance effect at the sweeping rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>k). The contribution of capacitance increases from ≈74% to ≈89% with the sweeping rate ranging from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>l). As a contrast, as plotted in Figure S13 in the Supporting Information, the CV curves of O3-NaNM electrode still maintain four pairs of redox peaks at various sweeping rates and corresponding <i>b</i> parameters are calculated as 0.693 and 0.524, respectively, which suggest that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage is governed by a diffusion controlled process. To grasp more insights into kinetic properties, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements were employed to investigate O3-NaLNMCM electrode.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0035_R_d76141122e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a>, <a href="#adma201803765-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0036_R_d76141122e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Res.">36</a></span> According to the GITT profile (Figure S14, Supporting Information), the obtained maximum specific capacity is 148.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and the equilibrium voltage shows a continuous change between 2.6 and 4.0 V, which indicates the occurrence of a solid-solution reaction process in this voltage range. The curves show a small average voltage polarization (68.9 mV) and ohmic polarization (32.8 mV) during the whole charge process (Figure S15, Supporting Information). In addition, the O3-NaLNMCM electrode delivers an outstanding capacity retention of 91.9% after 600 cycles with a high and stable coulombic efficiency even at a high rate of 5C (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0002">2</a>m). By comparison, the O3-NaNM electrode exhibits very fast capacity and energy efficiency declines with midpoint voltage decay and severe fluctuation of coulombic efficiency (Figures S16−S18, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201803765-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/78c45b33-1bc7-4225-8b18-3ebc506fa79c/adma201803765-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/78c45b33-1bc7-4225-8b18-3ebc506fa79c/adma201803765-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/89a70d98-3e4d-4be3-97ef-6411f3be86a9/adma201803765-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/78c45b33-1bc7-4225-8b18-3ebc506fa79c/adma201803765-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201803765-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201803765" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM electrodes. a,b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves versus specific capacity of O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM electrodes at 0.5C in the voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V. c,d) CV of O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM electrodes at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. e,f) Rate performance of O3-NaNM electrode at various rates and corresponding galvanostatic charge/discharge curves versus specific capacity. g,h) Rate performance of O3-NaLNMCM electrode at various rates and corresponding galvanostatic charge/discharge curves versus specific capacity. i,j) CV of O3-NaLNMCM electrode at different scan rates and corresponding linear fitting of the log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>) plots at oxidation and reduction peaks. k) Fitted pseudo-capacitive contribution (red area) of O3-NaLNMCM electrode at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. l) Ratio of the pseudo-capacitive (red) and diffusion-controlled (black) capacities of O3-NaLNMCM electrode at different scan rates. m) Cycling performance of O3-NaLNMCM electrode during 600 cycles at 5C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The combined analyses of ex situ XAS (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0003">3</a>) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Figure S19, Supporting Information) were carried out to deeply understand the charge compensation mechanism of O3-NaLNMCM. Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0003">3</a>a–c displays the normalized ex situ X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) at Ni, Mn, and Cu K-edges of O3-NaLNMCM at different charge/discharge states. The Ni K-edge absorption spectrum obviously shifts toward the higher-energy side as charge increases from 2.0 to 4.0 V, which demonstrates the oxidation of Ni<sup>2+</sup> to Ni<sup>3+</sup> according to the previous study.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0037_R_d76141122e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Inorg. Chem.">37</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0040_R_d76141122e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Mater.">40</a></span> In contrast, the Mn K-edge does not display clear change and the slight chemical shift is ascribed to the change in the local coordinational environment of manganese, indicating that manganese does not participate in the redox reaction. Surprisingly, the Cu K-edge clearly shifts to the higher-energy region with obvious shape change after Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0004">4</a>b), indicating Cu<sup>2+</sup> is oxidized to a higher valence state. Therefore, it can be rationally deduced that Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox couples are simultaneously involved in the charge compensation mechanism over the potential window of 2.0–4.0 V, which is highly consistent with the similar discharge specific capacity between O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM electrodes at 0.5C. In addition, the interatomic distance information of O3-NaLNMCM was investigated via extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra at Ni, Mn, and Cu K-edges (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0003">3</a>d–f). The two intense peaks over a range of 1–3 Å can be assigned to a TM–O first coordination and a TM–TM second coordination. As Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from the crystal lattice, the changed interatomic distances of TM–O first coordination are detected at the Ni and Cu K-edge, respectively, whereas the Mn K-edge spectra has almost no change. The second TM–TM coordination shells of Ni, Mn, and Cu K-edges exhibit similar shifts of decreased TM–TM interatomic distances after charging to 4 V, corresponding to the change of in-plane interatomic distance caused by O3–P3 phase transition, which matches well with the results of in situ XRD patterns in the latter parts.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201803765-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cee4f8ea-27eb-4ad0-9e19-de0fb2444758/adma201803765-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cee4f8ea-27eb-4ad0-9e19-de0fb2444758/adma201803765-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6f19a99b-0b45-4002-876a-adf3589dbc95/adma201803765-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cee4f8ea-27eb-4ad0-9e19-de0fb2444758/adma201803765-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201803765-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201803765" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Charge compensation mechanism of O3-NaLNMCM upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. a–c) Ex situ XANES spectra at the Ni K-edge, Mn K-edge, and Cu K-edge of O3-NaLNMCM electrode collected at different charge/discharge states, and d–f) corresponding ex situ EXAFS spectra at the Ni K-edge, Mn K-edge, and Cu K-edge.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201803765-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cf7f3b44-0ea9-40e9-ae9b-0b95336115fc/adma201803765-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cf7f3b44-0ea9-40e9-ae9b-0b95336115fc/adma201803765-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/73b13adc-823d-42bf-bf46-4fdd551e73f3/adma201803765-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cf7f3b44-0ea9-40e9-ae9b-0b95336115fc/adma201803765-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201803765-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201803765" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Crystal structural evolution of O3-NaLNMCM upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. a) In situ XRD patterns collected during the first and second charge/discharge at 0.1C in the voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V. Black asterisks represent peaks from Al window. b) Corresponding intensity contour maps (bird's eye view) concerning the evolution of the main characteristic diffraction peaks.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further study the structure evolution of the O3-NaNM and O3-NaLNMCM upon Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation process, in situ XRD experiments were executed and corresponding intensity contour maps (bird's eye view) could clearly demonstrate the structural evolution (Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0004">4</a>a,b and Figure S20, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0041_R_d76141122e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Mater.">41</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0043_R_d76141122e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ. Sci.">43</a></span> When Na<sup>+</sup> is being extracted during the charge process, the (003) peaks of O3 phase consecutively shift to a lower angle and then split into two, revealing that P3 phase starts to appear via a two-phase reaction, which is consistent with voltage plateau region at 2.5–2.6 V in the electrochemical profile. It is noticed that the intensity of the (104)<sub>O3</sub> peak totally disappears and all diffraction patterns can be assigned to a hexagonal P3 phase upon further charging. The P3 phase peaks shift continuously without the formation of other new peaks until charged to 4 V and such phenomenon indicates a solid-solution reaction, which is in good agreement with the sloping curve. As Na<sup>+</sup> is being inserted back to the crystal lattice after full cycle (Figure S21, Supporting Information), the XRD patterns experience an exact opposite evolution and the phase transition O3–P3 is highly reversible, which accounts for the remarkable cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to the superior high-rate capabilities and long-term cycling stability in half Na cells, it more significantly displays outstanding full batteries performance to preliminarily demonstrate the practicability of the O3-NaLNMCM cathode material (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0005">5</a>a).<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0044_R_d76141122e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#adma201803765-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0045_R_d76141122e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></span> In advanced assembling of the full batteries, electrochemical properties of hard carbon were evaluated by CV and galvanostatic charging/discharging tests in half cells with metallic sodium foil as counter electrode, which is important for subsequent matching with O3-NaLNMCM electrode.<span><a href="#adma201803765-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0046_R_d76141122e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - RSC Adv.">46</a>-<a href="#adma201803765-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201803765-bib-0048_R_d76141122e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></span> The hard carbon exhibits a reversible specific capacity of 314.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over the potential window of 0.001–2.0 V, and from the corresponding CV curves as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0005">5</a>b and Figure S22 in the Supporting Information, it could reasonably be inferred that the average working voltage of full cell is about 2.9 V at 0.5C in full cell system. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0005">5</a>c,d, the full cells deliver a high specific capacity of 126.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C and corresponding energy density up to 371.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 1.9–3.9 V based on the mass of cathode (Figure S23, Supporting Information). Moreover, even when cycled at 20C, it still displays a reversible capacity of 77.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and the calculated specific power is about 6.8 kW kg<sup>−1</sup>. Besides, the capacity retention is still up to 87.8% after 200 cycles at 5C and corresponding galvanostatic charge/discharge curves at different cycles are plotted in Figure <a href="#adma201803765-fig-0005">5</a>e,f and Figure S24 in the Supporting Information. It is worth noting that the full cells show the stable midpoint voltage (≈2.7 V) and high energy efficiency (&gt;83%) during 200 cycles at 5C (Figures S25 and S26, Supporting Information). Other detailed electrochemical parameters at different current densities are comprehensively summarized in Table S5 in the Supporting Information, which demonstrate the promising applications of hard carbon//O3-NaLNMCM for large-scale energy storage.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201803765-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6badf8f8-8e9b-4af1-b83f-1576ff3082bc/adma201803765-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6badf8f8-8e9b-4af1-b83f-1576ff3082bc/adma201803765-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/18c2e69b-ea8a-4d1a-b303-51ab0bcc5af4/adma201803765-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6badf8f8-8e9b-4af1-b83f-1576ff3082bc/adma201803765-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201803765-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201803765" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of full cell system coupling O3-NaLNMCM as cathode material and hard carbon as anode material. a) Schematic showing the discharge process. b) CV and galvanostatic charge/discharge curves versus specific capacity of O3-NaLNMCM and hard carbon electrodes. c,d) Rate performance at various rates and corresponding galvanostatic charge/discharge curves versus specific capacity in the voltage range of 1.9–3.9 V. e) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves versus specific capacity in 5th, 20th, 50th, 80th, 100th, 150th, and 200th cycles at 5C. f) Cycling performance of full cell system during 200 cycles at 5C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, an O3-type Na[Li<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> material with the exposed {010} active facets by multiple-layer oriented stacking nanosheets is successfully constructed via the reasonable structure design and chemical substitution. On one hand, the unique structure of cathode material could not only efficiently facilitate sodium-ion transport due to the short diffusion distances, high electrode–electrolyte contact area, and increased thickness of {010} active facets, but also it was beneficial for a higher capacitive contribution. On the other hand, chemical substitution suppresses the unfavorable multiphase transformation in the high voltage regions and retains a highly reversible O3–P3 phase transition over a wide capacity range. Moreover, both Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox couples are simultaneously involved in the charge compensation mechanism. As a result, the electrode delivers an excellent discharge capacity of 132.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and high-energy density of 397.5 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C, outstanding rate performance (71.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 16.9 kW kg<sup>−1</sup> at 50C), and remarkable cycling stability (91.9% capacity retention after 600 cycles at 5C). In addition, the cathode electrode displays a high discharge capacity of 126.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with energy density up to 371.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C, superior rate performance (77.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 6.8 kW kg<sup>−1</sup> at 20C), and splendid cycling stability (87.8% capacity retention after 200 cycles at 5C) in full cell system. These findings might open up the modulation of the chemical and physical properties in layered oxide cathode materials and push the commercialization of SIBs as a decisive step forward.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
